## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



In this chapter, we will be reviewing the fundamental concepts of dynamics and control covered in the course 2.003 at MIT. This chapter will serve as a brief refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who are new to the subject.



### Section 18.1: Uniaxial Motion:



In this section, we will be reviewing the concept of uniaxial motion, which is the motion of an object along a single axis. This is a fundamental concept in dynamics and control, as many systems can be simplified to a uniaxial motion problem. 



#### 18.1a: Understanding Uniaxial Motion



Uniaxial motion can be described using the basic laws of motion, namely Newton's laws. According to Newton's first law, an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion along a straight line unless acted upon by an external force. This means that in the absence of any external forces, an object will continue to move at a constant velocity along a single axis. 



To fully understand uniaxial motion, we must also consider Newton's second law, which states that the acceleration of an object is directly proportional to the net force acting on it and inversely proportional to its mass. This can be expressed mathematically as:



$$

F = ma

$$



where F is the net force, m is the mass of the object, and a is the acceleration. In the case of uniaxial motion, the net force acting on the object is equal to the sum of all the forces acting along the single axis. 



In addition to Newton's laws, we can also use the concept of energy to analyze uniaxial motion. The total energy of a system is the sum of its kinetic and potential energies. In the case of uniaxial motion, the kinetic energy is given by:



$$

K = \frac{1}{2}mv^2

$$



where m is the mass of the object and v is its velocity. The potential energy is given by:



$$

U = mgh

$$



where m is the mass of the object, g is the acceleration due to gravity, and h is the height of the object. By considering the conservation of energy, we can analyze the motion of an object in terms of its energy.



In summary, understanding uniaxial motion is crucial for analyzing more complex systems in the field of dynamics and control. By applying Newton's laws and the concept of energy, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of objects moving along a single axis.

## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



In this chapter, we will be reviewing the fundamental concepts of dynamics and control covered in the course 2.003 at MIT. This chapter will serve as a brief refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who are new to the subject.



### Section 18.1: Uniaxial Motion:



In this section, we will be reviewing the concept of uniaxial motion, which is the motion of an object along a single axis. This is a fundamental concept in dynamics and control, as many systems can be simplified to a uniaxial motion problem. 



#### 18.1a: Understanding Uniaxial Motion



Uniaxial motion can be described using the basic laws of motion, namely Newton's laws. According to Newton's first law, an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion along a straight line unless acted upon by an external force. This means that in the absence of any external forces, an object will continue to move at a constant velocity along a single axis. 



To fully understand uniaxial motion, we must also consider Newton's second law, which states that the acceleration of an object is directly proportional to the net force acting on it and inversely proportional to its mass. This can be expressed mathematically as:



$$

F = ma

$$



where F is the net force, m is the mass of the object, and a is the acceleration. In the case of uniaxial motion, the net force acting on the object is equal to the sum of all the forces acting along the single axis. 



In addition to Newton's laws, we can also use the concept of energy to analyze uniaxial motion. Energy is a fundamental concept in physics and is defined as the ability to do work. In the case of uniaxial motion, we can use the concept of kinetic energy, which is the energy an object possesses due to its motion. Kinetic energy is defined as:



$$

K = \frac{1}{2}mv^2

$$



where m is the mass of the object and v is its velocity. This equation shows that the kinetic energy of an object is directly proportional to its mass and the square of its velocity. 



Another important concept in uniaxial motion is potential energy, which is the energy an object possesses due to its position or configuration. In the case of a spring-mass system, the potential energy is given by:



$$

U = \frac{1}{2}kx^2

$$



where k is the spring constant and x is the displacement of the mass from its equilibrium position. This equation shows that the potential energy of the system is directly proportional to the spring constant and the square of the displacement. 



By understanding the concepts of energy and how they relate to uniaxial motion, we can better analyze and control systems in this type of motion. In the next section, we will apply these concepts to second order systems.

## Chapter 18: Brief Review of 2.003:



In this chapter, we will be reviewing the fundamental concepts of dynamics and control covered in the course 2.003 at MIT. This chapter will serve as a brief refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who are new to the subject.



### Section 18.1: Uniaxial Motion:



In this section, we will be reviewing the concept of uniaxial motion, which is the motion of an object along a single axis. This is a fundamental concept in dynamics and control, as many systems can be simplified to a uniaxial motion problem. 



#### 18.1a: Understanding Uniaxial Motion



Uniaxial motion can be described using the basic laws of motion, namely Newton's laws. According to Newton's first law, an object will remain at rest or in uniform motion along a straight line unless acted upon by an external force. This means that in the absence of any external forces, an object will continue to move at a constant velocity along a single axis. 



To fully understand uniaxial motion, we must also consider Newton's second law, which states that the acceleration of an object is directly proportional to the net force acting on it and inversely proportional to its mass. This can be expressed mathematically as:



$$

F = ma

$$



where F is the net force, m is the mass of the object, and a is the acceleration. In the case of uniaxial motion, the net force acting on the object is equal to the sum of all the forces acting along the single axis. 



In addition to Newton's laws, we can also use the concept of energy to analyze uniaxial motion. The total energy of a system is the sum of its kinetic and potential energies. In the case of uniaxial motion, the kinetic energy is given by:



$$

K = \frac{1}{2}mv^2

$$



where m is the mass of the object and v is its velocity. The potential energy is given by:



$$

U = mgh

$$



where m is the mass of the object, g is the acceleration due to gravity, and h is the height of the object. By considering the conservation of energy, we can analyze the motion of an object in terms of its energy.



### Section 18.2: First Order Systems:



In this section, we will be discussing first order systems, which are systems that can be described by a first order differential equation. These systems are commonly used in modeling and control applications.



#### 18.2a: Understanding First Order Systems



A first order system can be described by the following differential equation:



$$

\frac{dx}{dt} = ax + bu

$$



where x is the state variable, t is time, a is a constant, b is a constant, and u is the input. This equation represents the rate of change of the state variable x over time. The input u can be a force, voltage, or any other variable that affects the system.



To solve this differential equation, we can use the method of separation of variables. This involves isolating the variables on either side of the equation and integrating both sides. The solution will depend on the initial conditions of the system.



### Section 18.3: Second Order Systems:



In this section, we will be discussing second order systems, which are systems that can be described by a second order differential equation. These systems are commonly used in modeling and control applications.



#### 18.3a: Understanding Second Order Systems



A second order system can be described by the following differential equation:



$$

\frac{d^2x}{dt^2} = ax + bu

$$



where x is the state variable, t is time, a is a constant, b is a constant, and u is the input. This equation represents the acceleration of the state variable x over time. Similar to first order systems, the input u can be a force, voltage, or any other variable that affects the system.



To solve this differential equation, we can use the method of undetermined coefficients or the method of Laplace transforms. The solution will also depend on the initial conditions of the system.



### Subsection 18.3c: Comparing First and Second Order Systems



While both first and second order systems can be used to model and control various systems, there are some key differences between them. First order systems are simpler and easier to analyze, but they are limited in their ability to accurately represent more complex systems. Second order systems, on the other hand, can accurately model more complex systems, but they are more difficult to analyze and control.



In terms of control, first order systems are typically used for simple systems that require basic control, while second order systems are used for more complex systems that require more advanced control techniques. It is important to understand the differences between these two types of systems in order to choose the appropriate modeling and control techniques for a given system.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction



In the previous chapter, we discussed the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. In this chapter, we will expand our understanding to more complex systems by exploring the plane-motion dynamics of rigid bodies. This topic is crucial in the field of engineering as it allows us to analyze and design systems with multiple degrees of freedom, such as robots, vehicles, and aircraft.



The motion of a rigid body is characterized by its translation and rotation in three-dimensional space. To accurately model and control this motion, we need to understand the forces and torques acting on the body, as well as the relationships between its position, velocity, and acceleration. This chapter will cover the equations of motion for rigid bodies, including the Newton-Euler equations and the Euler-Lagrange equations. We will also discuss the concept of inertia and its role in determining the dynamics of a rigid body.



Furthermore, we will explore the different types of motion that a rigid body can exhibit, such as translation, rotation, and general plane motion. We will also discuss the concept of rigid body kinematics, which deals with the relationships between the body's position, velocity, and acceleration without considering the forces and torques acting on it. This will provide us with a foundation for understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies.



Overall, this chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. By the end, readers will have the necessary tools to model and analyze complex systems with multiple degrees of freedom, making it a valuable resource for students and professionals in the field of engineering.

# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



### Section 19.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies



In this section, we will build upon the concepts introduced in the previous section and delve deeper into the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it, and it is an essential aspect of understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies.



#### Subsection 19.2a: Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



Before we can analyze the dynamics of a rigid body, we must first understand its motion. In this subsection, we will discuss the key concepts of position, velocity, and acceleration as they relate to rigid bodies in plane motion.



Position is a fundamental concept in kinematics, and it describes the location of an object in space. In the case of a rigid body, its position can be described by the position of its center of mass and its orientation in space. We will explore how to mathematically represent these quantities and how they change over time.



Velocity is the rate of change of position, and it is a vector quantity that includes both magnitude and direction. In the case of a rigid body, its velocity can be described by the velocity of its center of mass and its angular velocity. We will discuss how these quantities are related and how they can be calculated.



Acceleration is the rate of change of velocity, and it is also a vector quantity. In the case of a rigid body, its acceleration can be described by the acceleration of its center of mass and its angular acceleration. We will explore how these quantities are related and how they can be calculated using Newton's second law.



In addition to these key concepts, we will also discuss the concept of frames of reference and how they are used to describe the motion of a rigid body. We will also introduce the concept of relative motion, which is essential in understanding the motion of multiple rigid bodies in a system.



By the end of this subsection, you will have a solid understanding of the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion, which will serve as the foundation for our discussions on dynamics and control in the following sections. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



### Section 19.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of kinematics and how it relates to rigid bodies in plane motion. In this section, we will build upon those concepts and explore some applications of plane-motion kinematics.



#### Subsection 19.2b: Applications of Plane-motion Kinematics



In this subsection, we will discuss some practical applications of plane-motion kinematics. These applications are essential in understanding the dynamics of rigid bodies and are commonly used in engineering and physics.



One application of plane-motion kinematics is in the design and analysis of mechanical systems. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies, engineers can predict the motion of a system and design it to perform specific tasks. For example, in robotics, the kinematics of rigid bodies is used to design and control the motion of robotic arms.



Another application is in the study of celestial mechanics. The motion of planets, moons, and other celestial bodies can be described using the principles of plane-motion kinematics. By understanding the kinematics of these bodies, scientists can predict their future positions and movements.



In addition to these practical applications, plane-motion kinematics is also essential in understanding the motion of fluids. By applying the principles of kinematics, scientists and engineers can study the flow of fluids and design systems to control and manipulate them.



Furthermore, the concepts of frames of reference and relative motion, which were introduced in the previous section, are crucial in understanding the motion of multiple bodies in a system. By using different frames of reference, we can simplify the analysis of complex systems and better understand the interactions between different bodies.



Overall, the applications of plane-motion kinematics are vast and diverse, and they play a crucial role in many fields of study. By understanding the kinematics of rigid bodies, we can better understand the world around us and design systems that can perform specific tasks with precision and accuracy. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



### Section 19.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of kinematics and how it relates to rigid bodies in plane motion. In this section, we will build upon those concepts and explore some applications of plane-motion kinematics.



#### Subsection 19.2c: Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



While the applications of plane-motion kinematics are vast and diverse, there are also some challenges that arise when dealing with the motion of rigid bodies. In this subsection, we will discuss some of these challenges and how they can be addressed.



One challenge is dealing with non-uniform motion. In the previous section, we assumed that the motion of rigid bodies was uniform, meaning that the velocity and acceleration were constant. However, in real-world scenarios, this is often not the case. Rigid bodies can experience non-uniform motion due to external forces or changes in their mass distribution. To address this challenge, we can use calculus to analyze the motion of non-uniform rigid bodies.



Another challenge is dealing with multiple degrees of freedom. In the previous section, we focused on the motion of rigid bodies in a single plane. However, in many practical applications, rigid bodies can have multiple degrees of freedom, meaning they can move in more than one plane. This adds complexity to the analysis of their motion and requires the use of advanced mathematical techniques such as vector calculus and differential equations.



Furthermore, the assumptions made in plane-motion kinematics may not always hold true. For example, we assumed that the rigid body was perfectly rigid and did not deform under external forces. However, in reality, all bodies have some level of flexibility, and this can affect their motion. To address this challenge, we can use more advanced models that take into account the flexibility of the body.



In addition to these challenges, there may also be limitations in the accuracy of our measurements and calculations. Small errors in measurements or assumptions can lead to significant discrepancies in the predicted motion of a rigid body. To mitigate this, we can use error analysis techniques and conduct sensitivity studies to understand the impact of these errors on our results.



Overall, while plane-motion kinematics provides a useful framework for understanding the motion of rigid bodies, it is essential to be aware of these challenges and address them appropriately in our analysis. By doing so, we can improve the accuracy and applicability of our models and better understand the dynamics of rigid bodies in motion.





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



### Section 19.3: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will build upon those concepts and explore the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Dynamics is the study of the forces and torques that cause motion, and it is essential in understanding the behavior of rigid bodies.



#### Subsection 19.3a: Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion, we must first understand the concept of inertia. Inertia is the resistance of an object to changes in its motion. In other words, it is the tendency of an object to maintain its state of motion. The inertia of a rigid body is dependent on its mass and distribution of mass.



When a rigid body is in motion, it experiences forces and torques that cause changes in its motion. These forces and torques can be external, such as gravity or contact forces, or internal, such as friction or tension. To analyze the motion of a rigid body, we must consider all of these forces and torques and their effects on the body.



One of the fundamental principles in dynamics is Newton's Second Law, which states that the net force acting on an object is equal to its mass multiplied by its acceleration. In the case of a rigid body, this law can be extended to include rotational motion, where the net torque acting on the body is equal to its moment of inertia multiplied by its angular acceleration.



To apply Newton's Second Law to rigid bodies in plane motion, we must first define a coordinate system and choose a point about which we will calculate the moments of inertia and torques. This point is known as the center of mass, and it simplifies the analysis of the body's motion.



Another important concept in dynamics is conservation of momentum. In a closed system, the total momentum remains constant, meaning that the sum of the momenta of all objects in the system does not change. This principle can be applied to rigid bodies in plane motion, where the linear and angular momenta are conserved separately.



In addition to external forces and torques, rigid bodies can also experience internal forces and torques due to the interaction between different parts of the body. These internal forces and torques can affect the body's motion and must be considered in the analysis.



In the next subsection, we will explore some applications of plane-motion dynamics and see how these principles can be applied to real-world scenarios. 





# Title: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



## Chapter 19: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



### Section 19.3: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will build upon those concepts and explore the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Dynamics is the study of the forces and torques that cause motion, and it is essential in understanding the behavior of rigid bodies.



#### Subsection 19.3a: Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



To understand the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion, we must first understand the concept of inertia. Inertia is the resistance of an object to changes in its motion. In other words, it is the tendency of an object to maintain its state of motion. The inertia of a rigid body is dependent on its mass and distribution of mass.



When a rigid body is in motion, it experiences forces and torques that cause changes in its motion. These forces and torques can be external, such as gravity or contact forces, or internal, such as friction or tension. To analyze the motion of a rigid body, we must consider all of these forces and torques and their effects on the body.



One of the fundamental principles in dynamics is Newton's Second Law, which states that the net force acting on an object is equal to its mass multiplied by its acceleration. In the case of a rigid body, this law can be extended to include rotational motion, where the net torque acting on the body is equal to its moment of inertia multiplied by its angular acceleration.



To apply Newton's Second Law to rigid bodies in plane motion, we must first define a coordinate system and choose a point about which we will calculate the moments of inertia and torques. This point is known as the center of mass, and it simplifies the analysis of the body's motion.



Another important concept in dynamics is conservation of momentum. In a closed system, the total momentum remains constant, meaning that the sum of the momenta of all objects in the system remains constant. This principle can be applied to rigid bodies in plane motion, where the total momentum is equal to the product of the mass and velocity of the center of mass.



In addition to conservation of momentum, we can also apply the principle of conservation of angular momentum to rigid bodies in plane motion. This principle states that the total angular momentum of a system remains constant unless acted upon by an external torque. This can be useful in analyzing the motion of a rigid body, as it allows us to determine the final angular velocity of the body after a collision or other event.



In this section, we will also explore the concept of work and energy in the context of rigid bodies in plane motion. Work is defined as the product of a force and the distance over which it acts, and it is closely related to the concept of energy. In the case of rigid bodies, we can consider both translational and rotational work, which can be used to determine the change in kinetic energy of the body.



Finally, we will discuss the concept of stability in rigid bodies in plane motion. A body is considered stable if it returns to its original position after being disturbed. We can analyze the stability of a rigid body by considering the forces and torques acting on it and determining its equilibrium points.



In the next section, we will apply these concepts to various examples and problems in order to gain a deeper understanding of the dynamics of rigid bodies in plane motion.

## Chapter: Modeling Dynamics and Control II



### Introduction:



In the previous chapter, we explored the fundamentals of modeling dynamics and control for single-degree-of-freedom systems. We learned how to represent the behavior of a system using mathematical equations and how to design control systems to regulate its response. In this chapter, we will expand our understanding to more complex systems with two degrees of freedom. These systems are characterized by two independent variables that govern their behavior, making their analysis and control more challenging.



We will begin by discussing the concept of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. Vibrations are a common phenomenon in mechanical and structural systems, and understanding their behavior is crucial for designing stable and efficient systems. We will explore the different types of vibrations and their characteristics, such as natural frequency, damping, and resonance. We will also learn how to model these vibrations using mathematical equations and how to analyze their response.



Next, we will delve into the dynamics of two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will learn how to represent the behavior of these systems using state-space equations and how to analyze their stability. We will also explore the concept of transfer functions and how they can be used to design control systems for two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will cover topics such as pole placement, root locus, and frequency response analysis.



Finally, we will apply our knowledge of modeling and control to real-world examples. We will examine case studies of two-degree-of-freedom systems in various fields, such as mechanical engineering, aerospace, and robotics. We will see how the principles and techniques we have learned can be applied to solve practical problems and improve the performance of these systems.



By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. You will be able to model their behavior, analyze their response, and design control systems to regulate their behavior. This knowledge will be invaluable in your future endeavors in the field of engineering and beyond. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of vibrations and dynamics in two-degree-of-freedom systems.

### Section 20.1: Brief Review of 2.003:



In this section, we will provide a brief review of the concepts covered in 2.003, the introductory course to modeling dynamics and control at MIT. This will serve as a refresher for those who have taken the course and as an introduction for those who have not.



2.003 is a foundational course that covers the principles of modeling, analysis, and control of dynamic systems. It introduces students to the fundamental concepts and techniques used in the field of dynamics and control, such as differential equations, transfer functions, and stability analysis.



The course begins by discussing the concept of a dynamic system and its components, such as inputs, outputs, and states. It then moves on to cover the different types of systems, including mechanical, electrical, and thermal systems, and how to model their behavior using mathematical equations.



Next, the course delves into the analysis of dynamic systems. Students learn how to solve differential equations using analytical and numerical methods and how to analyze the response of a system to different inputs. They also learn about the concept of stability and how to determine the stability of a system using techniques such as Routh-Hurwitz and Nyquist stability criteria.



The final part of the course focuses on control systems. Students learn about the different types of control systems, such as open-loop and closed-loop, and how to design them to regulate the behavior of a dynamic system. They also learn about the concept of feedback and how it can be used to improve the performance of a control system.



Overall, 2.003 provides students with a solid foundation in the principles and techniques of modeling dynamics and control. It prepares them for more advanced courses, such as this one, where they will apply these concepts to more complex systems with multiple degrees of freedom. ## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In this section, we will build upon the concepts covered in the previous section and apply them to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. Specifically, we will focus on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion and how it relates to the dynamics and control of these systems.



#### 20.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



Before we dive into the specifics of plane-motion kinematics, let's first review the concept of kinematics in general. Kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. In other words, it focuses on the position, velocity, and acceleration of a body without taking into account the forces acting on it.



In the case of rigid bodies, kinematics is concerned with the motion of the body as a whole, rather than the individual particles that make it up. This is because rigid bodies are assumed to be made up of particles that are fixed in relation to each other, meaning they maintain a constant distance and orientation.



Now, let's apply this concept to the study of plane-motion kinematics. Plane motion refers to the motion of a rigid body in a two-dimensional plane. This is a common scenario in engineering systems, where the motion of a body is constrained to a specific plane.



To describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we use a set of coordinates known as the body-fixed coordinate system. This system is attached to the body and moves with it, allowing us to track the position, velocity, and acceleration of the body as it moves.



The body-fixed coordinate system consists of two perpendicular axes, typically labeled x and y, and a reference point known as the origin. The position of any point on the body can be described using these coordinates, with the origin serving as the reference point.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we need to consider both translation and rotation. Translation refers to the movement of the body as a whole, while rotation refers to the change in orientation of the body.



Translation can be described using the position vector, which represents the distance and direction of a point from the origin. Rotation, on the other hand, can be described using the orientation of the body, typically represented by the angle of rotation.



In the next section, we will apply these concepts to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. We will see how the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion plays a crucial role in understanding the dynamics and control of these systems.





## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of kinematics and how it applies to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. Now, we will delve deeper into the topic by focusing on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion.



#### 20.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



As mentioned before, kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. In the case of rigid bodies, kinematics focuses on the motion of the body as a whole, rather than the individual particles that make it up. This is because rigid bodies are assumed to be made up of particles that are fixed in relation to each other, meaning they maintain a constant distance and orientation.



In the study of plane-motion kinematics, we are concerned with the motion of a rigid body in a two-dimensional plane. This is a common scenario in engineering systems, where the motion of a body is constrained to a specific plane. Examples of such systems include pendulums, car suspensions, and aircraft wings.



To describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we use a set of coordinates known as the body-fixed coordinate system. This system is attached to the body and moves with it, allowing us to track the position, velocity, and acceleration of the body as it moves.



The body-fixed coordinate system consists of two perpendicular axes, typically labeled x and y, and a reference point known as the origin. The position of any point on the body can be described using these coordinates, with the origin serving as the reference point.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we need to consider both translational and rotational motion. Translational motion refers to the movement of the body's center of mass, while rotational motion refers to the rotation of the body about its center of mass.



In the next section, we will explore the equations and principles that govern the plane-motion kinematics of rigid bodies and how they relate to the dynamics and control of two-degree-of-freedom systems. 





## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.2: Plane-motion Kinematics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the basics of kinematics and how it applies to the study of vibrations in two-degree-of-freedom systems. Now, we will delve deeper into the topic by focusing on the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion.



#### 20.2a Understanding Plane-motion Kinematics



As mentioned before, kinematics is the study of motion without considering the forces that cause it. In the case of rigid bodies, kinematics focuses on the motion of the body as a whole, rather than the individual particles that make it up. This is because rigid bodies are assumed to be made up of particles that are fixed in relation to each other, meaning they maintain a constant distance and orientation.



In the study of plane-motion kinematics, we are concerned with the motion of a rigid body in a two-dimensional plane. This is a common scenario in engineering systems, where the motion of a body is constrained to a specific plane. Examples of such systems include pendulums, car suspensions, and aircraft wings.



To describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we use a set of coordinates known as the body-fixed coordinate system. This system is attached to the body and moves with it, allowing us to track the position, velocity, and acceleration of the body as it moves.



The body-fixed coordinate system consists of two perpendicular axes, typically labeled x and y, and a reference point known as the origin. The position of any point on the body can be described using these coordinates, with the origin serving as the reference point.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we need to consider both translational and rotational motion. Translational motion refers to the movement of the body's center of mass, while rotational motion refers to the rotation of the body about its center of mass.



#### 20.2b Equations of Motion for Plane-motion Kinematics



To analyze the motion of a rigid body in a plane, we need to derive the equations of motion that govern its translational and rotational motion. These equations can be derived using Newton's second law of motion, which states that the sum of all forces acting on a body is equal to its mass times its acceleration.



For translational motion, we can write the equation of motion as:



$$

\sum F = m\ddot{x}

$$



Where $\sum F$ represents the sum of all forces acting on the body, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\ddot{x}$ is the acceleration of the body's center of mass.



For rotational motion, we can write the equation of motion as:



$$

\sum M = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



Where $\sum M$ represents the sum of all moments acting on the body, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body about its center of mass, and $\ddot{\theta}$ is the angular acceleration of the body.



#### 20.2c Challenges in Plane-motion Kinematics



While the equations of motion for plane-motion kinematics may seem straightforward, there are several challenges that arise when applying them to real-world systems. One of the main challenges is accurately modeling the forces and moments acting on the body. In many cases, these forces and moments may be difficult to measure or may vary over time, making it challenging to accurately predict the motion of the body.



Another challenge is dealing with non-rigid bodies, where the assumption of fixed particles may not hold true. In these cases, more complex equations of motion must be derived to accurately describe the motion of the body.



In the next section, we will explore some techniques for solving these challenges and accurately modeling the motion of two-degree-of-freedom systems.





## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.3: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will move on to the dynamics of these systems, which involves considering the forces that cause the motion.



#### 20.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



In the study of dynamics, we are concerned with the forces and torques that act on a body and how they affect its motion. In the case of rigid bodies, these forces and torques can cause both translational and rotational motion.



To fully understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to consider the equations of motion for both translational and rotational motion. These equations can be derived using Newton's laws of motion and the principles of conservation of momentum and angular momentum.



For translational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum F = m\ddot{x}

$$



where $\sum F$ represents the sum of all external forces acting on the body, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\ddot{x}$ is the acceleration of the body's center of mass.



For rotational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum \tau = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



where $\sum \tau$ represents the sum of all external torques acting on the body, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body, and $\ddot{\theta}$ is the angular acceleration of the body.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to solve these equations simultaneously. This can be done using techniques such as the Lagrangian method or the Newton-Euler equations.



In the next section, we will apply these equations of motion to specific examples of two-degree-of-freedom systems and analyze their vibrations. 





## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.3: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will move on to the dynamics of these systems, which involves considering the forces that cause the motion.



#### 20.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



In the study of dynamics, we are concerned with the forces and torques that act on a body and how they affect its motion. In the case of rigid bodies, these forces and torques can cause both translational and rotational motion.



To fully understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to consider the equations of motion for both translational and rotational motion. These equations can be derived using Newton's laws of motion and the principles of conservation of momentum and angular momentum.



For translational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum F = m\ddot{x}

$$



where $\sum F$ represents the sum of all external forces acting on the body, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\ddot{x}$ is the acceleration of the body's center of mass.



For rotational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum \tau = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



where $\sum \tau$ represents the sum of all external torques acting on the body, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body, and $\ddot{\theta}$ is the angular acceleration of the body.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to solve these equations simultaneously. This can be done using techniques such as the Lagrangian method or the Newton-Euler equations.



#### 20.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



The equations of motion for translational and rotational motion can be applied to a variety of real-world systems. In this subsection, we will explore some common applications of plane-motion dynamics.



One example is the motion of a pendulum, which can be modeled as a rigid body in plane motion. The equations of motion can be used to analyze the motion of the pendulum and determine its period and amplitude of oscillation.



Another application is the motion of a rotating wheel, such as a bicycle wheel. By considering the forces and torques acting on the wheel, we can predict its motion and stability.



In engineering, plane-motion dynamics is also used to design and analyze mechanical systems, such as engines and turbines. By understanding the forces and torques involved, engineers can optimize the performance and efficiency of these systems.



In the next section, we will apply these equations of motion to specific examples of two-degree-of-freedom systems and analyze their vibrations. This will further demonstrate the practical applications of plane-motion dynamics in real-world systems.





## Chapter 20: Vibrations of Two-Degree-of-Freedom Systems:



### Section 20.3: Plane-motion Dynamics of Rigid Bodies:



In the previous section, we discussed the kinematics of rigid bodies in plane motion. Now, we will move on to the dynamics of these systems, which involves considering the forces that cause the motion.



#### 20.3a Understanding Plane-motion Dynamics



In the study of dynamics, we are concerned with the forces and torques that act on a body and how they affect its motion. In the case of rigid bodies, these forces and torques can cause both translational and rotational motion.



To fully understand the dynamics of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to consider the equations of motion for both translational and rotational motion. These equations can be derived using Newton's laws of motion and the principles of conservation of momentum and angular momentum.



For translational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum F = m\ddot{x}

$$



where $\sum F$ represents the sum of all external forces acting on the body, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $\ddot{x}$ is the acceleration of the body's center of mass.



For rotational motion, the equation of motion is given by:



$$

\sum \tau = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



where $\sum \tau$ represents the sum of all external torques acting on the body, $I$ is the moment of inertia of the body, and $\ddot{\theta}$ is the angular acceleration of the body.



To fully describe the motion of a rigid body in plane motion, we need to solve these equations simultaneously. This can be done using techniques such as the Lagrangian method or the Newton-Euler equations.



#### 20.3b Applications of Plane-motion Dynamics



The equations of motion for translational and rotational motion can be applied to a variety of real-world systems. In this subsection, we will explore some common applications of plane-motion dynamics.



One example is the motion of a pendulum, which can be modeled as a rigid body in plane motion. The equations of motion for a pendulum can be derived using the principles of conservation of energy and angular momentum. By considering the forces acting on the pendulum, we can write the following equations:



$$

\sum F = mg\sin\theta = m\ddot{x}

$$



$$

\sum \tau = -mg\cos\theta\cdot\frac{L}{2} = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



where $m$ is the mass of the pendulum, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\theta$ is the angle of the pendulum, $L$ is the length of the pendulum, and $I$ is the moment of inertia of the pendulum. These equations can then be solved to determine the motion of the pendulum.



Another example is the motion of a rotating wheel, which can also be modeled as a rigid body in plane motion. In this case, the equations of motion can be derived using the principles of conservation of energy and angular momentum, as well as the relationship between linear and angular velocity. By considering the forces acting on the wheel, we can write the following equations:



$$

\sum F = mg\sin\theta = m\ddot{x}

$$



$$

\sum \tau = -mg\cos\theta\cdot\frac{R}{2} = I\ddot{\theta}

$$



where $m$ is the mass of the wheel, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\theta$ is the angle of the wheel, $R$ is the radius of the wheel, and $I$ is the moment of inertia of the wheel. These equations can then be solved to determine the motion of the wheel.



#### 20.3c Challenges in Plane-motion Dynamics



While the equations of motion for translational and rotational motion may seem straightforward, there are several challenges that arise when applying them to real-world systems. One challenge is accurately modeling the forces and torques acting on a body, as they can be complex and difficult to measure. Another challenge is dealing with non-ideal conditions, such as friction and air resistance, which can significantly affect the motion of a body.



In addition, solving the equations of motion for a rigid body in plane motion can be mathematically complex, especially for systems with multiple degrees of freedom. This requires advanced mathematical techniques and can be time-consuming.



Despite these challenges, understanding plane-motion dynamics is crucial for analyzing and designing systems in various fields, such as engineering, physics, and robotics. By accurately modeling and predicting the motion of rigid bodies, we can better understand and control their behavior, leading to advancements in technology and innovation.
